style=\"min-height:1px!important;width:1px!important;border-width:0!important;margin-top:0!important;margin-bottom:0!important;margin-right:0!important;margin-left:0!important;padding-top:0!important;padding-bottom:0!important;padding-right:0!important;padding-left:0!important; \">","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 04 Nov 2020 12:15:00 +0000","feedId":54,"bgimg":"https://blog.hubspot.com/hubfs/How%20to%20Build%20an%20Email%20List%20from%20Scratch%2010%20Incredibly%20Effective%20Strategies-1.png","linkMd5":"f274b28afd8e9e2da56af561d2e4ce7a","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn18@2020_4/2020/11/14/17-30-18-525_12c755005a46bc43.webp","destWidth":1138,"destHeight":964,"sourceBytes":391944,"destBytes":391944,"author":"Caroline Forsey","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://blog.hubspot.com/hubfs/How%20to%20Build%20an%20Email%20List%20from%20Scratch%2010%20Incredibly%20Effective%20Strategies-1.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn18@2020_4/2020/11/14/17-30-18-525_12c755005a46bc43.webp","https://blog.hubspot.com/hubfs/How%20to%20Build%20an%20Email%20List%20from%20Scratch%2010%20Incredibly%20Effective%20Strategies.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn54@2020_3/2020/11/14/17-30-23-707_a7b3d7a86b566036.webp","https://blog.hubspot.com/hubfs/How%20to%20Build%20an%20Email%20List%20from%20Scratch%2010%20Incredibly%20Effective%20Strategies-2.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn32@2020_3/2020/11/14/17-30-20-601_e74b34f000c8d33e.webp","https://track.hubspot.com/__ptq.gif?a=53&k=14&r=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.hubspot.com%2Fmarketing%2Flist-building&bu=https%253A%252F%252Fblog.hubspot.com%252Fmarketing&bvt=rss":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn77@2020_3/2020/11/14/17-30-23-864_833b2473234bfb1a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605375018204},{"createdTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","updatedTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","title":"The Ultimate Guide to Brand Awareness","link":"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/brand-awareness","description":"<p>Have you ever heard people refer to themselves as “Apple people,” “Nike people,” or “Trader Joe’s” people?</p>  \n<p>This is what brand awareness can do for a brand: embed itself into consumer lifestyles and purchase habits so that they don’t have to think twice before becoming a customer — time and time again.</p> \n<p>This guide will help you better understand brand awareness, establish it among your audience, and build campaigns that allow it to continually grow and morph with your business. Let’s dive in.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<p>Brand awareness might seem like a vague concept, and in truth, it is. For those marketers and business owners out there who like to gauge success with neat and tidy numbers, brand awareness will likely ruffle your feathers.</p> \n<a></a> \n<p>But just because it isn’t a metric that can be perfectly determined doesn’t mean it doesn’t carry value. Brand awareness is incredibly important for business success and overall marketing goals. Here’s why.</p> \n<a></a> \n<h3>Brand awareness fosters trust.</h3> \n<a></a> \n<p>In a world where consumers rely on <a href=\"https://www.slideshare.net/HubSpot/the-hard-truth-about-marketing\">extensive research and others’ opinions</a> before making a purchase, brand trust is everything. Once a consumer bonds to your brand, they’re more likely to make repeat purchases with little to no forethought –– which then bridges the gap between trust and loyalty.</p> \n<p>Brand awareness establishes that brand trust. When you put a proverbial face to your brand name, consumers can trust easier. Brand awareness efforts give your brand a personality and outlet to be sincere, receive feedback, and tell a story. These are all ways that we, as humans, build trust with one another. The human/brand relationship isn’t any different.</p> \n<h3>Brand awareness creates association.</h3> \n<p>When you’ve had a paper cut, I bet you’ve put on a Band-Aid. When you had a pressing question, I’m sure you’ve Googled it. When you needed to make a few copies, I’m guessing that you Xeroxed them. And when you’ve packed for a nice picnic, I’m willing to bet you grabbed a Coke to drink.</p> \n<p>Am I correct? Most likely. But ... notice how the some of the words above are capitalized. These are brands, not nouns or verbs.</p> \n<p>Speaking in <i>brand-less</i> terms, Band-Aid should be referred to as bandage, Google, as a search engine, and Xerox as a copier. But it’s more fun to refer to the brand itself, even if we aren’t using their <i>specific</i> product.</p> \n<p>That’s what brand awareness does. It associates actions and products with particular brands, subconsciously encouraging us to replace common words with branded terms. And before you know it, simple paper cuts or picnics are doing the marketing for us.</p> \n<h3>Brand awareness builds brand equity.</h3> \n<p>Brand equity describes a brand’s value, which is determined by consumer experiences with and overall perception of the brand. Positive experiences and perception equal positive brand equity, and the same goes for negative notions.</p> \n<p>Here are a few valuable things that come from positive brand equity:</p> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Higher prices due to higher perceived value</li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\">A higher stock price</li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The ability to expand business through product or service line extensions</li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Greater social impact due to brand name value</li> \n</ul> \n<p>How does a brand establish (and increase) brand equity? By building brand awareness and consistently promoting positive experiences with the brand. Brand awareness is the foundation of brand equity.</p> \n<p>Once a consumer is aware of a brand, they start to recognize it without assistance, seek it out to make a purchase, begin to prefer it over other similar brands, and establish a loyalty that not only spurs on other purchases but also inspires recommendations to family and friends.</p> \n<p>That, my friends, is why brand awareness is so important. It establishes trust with your customers, creates positive associations, and builds invaluable brand equity that allows your brand to become a household name and consumer staple.</p> \n<a></a> \n<h2>How to Establish Brand Awareness</h2> \n<a></a> \n<p>Brand awareness among your audience and the general public doesn’t happen overnight. It also doesn’t happen from a simple advertisement or marketing campaign.</p> \n<p>Strong brand awareness is a result of multiple simultaneous efforts that extend beyond trying to get paying customers.</p> \n<p>If you expect to raise awareness of your brand by running a few product advertisements on Facebook, you won’t get very far. Not only will the consumer be focused on the product (not the brand), but the ad will also lack impact beyond a simple sale.</p> \n<p>Here are some ways to establish a solid brand awareness foundation and make a lasting impact with your audience:</p> \n<h3>Be a person, not a company.</h3> \n<p>When you get to know a new friend, what do you like to discover about them? I like to learn about hobbies, passions, likes and dislikes, and more. I also pay attention to how they speak, what they like to talk about, and what stuff gets them excited.</p> \n<p>These are the traits your brand should determine and promote about itself. To leave an impact with your audience, you’ve got to define yourself as more than a company that sells <i>stuff</i>. How else would you define yourself? What words would you use if you had to introduce your brand to a new friend?</p> \n<h3>Socialize.</h3> \n<p>Introvert or extrovert, outgoing or quiet, all humans benefit from social contact and spending time with one another. It’s how we stay connected, learn new things, and become known by others.</p> \n<p>The same goes for your brand. If you only attempt to connect with others when trying to make a sale or get support, you won’t be known as anything beyond a business with a singular intention (and the same goes for a person).</p> \n<a></a> \n<p>To raise awareness of your brand, <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/powerful-techniques-boost-brand-social-media-list\">you’ve got to be social</a>. Post on social media about things unrelated to your product or services. Interact with your audience by asking questions, commenting on posts, or retweeting or sharing content you like. Treat your social accounts as if you were a person trying to make friends, not a business trying to make money.</p> \n<p>Research shows that <a href=\"https://www.webershandwick.com/uploads/news/files/NA_SocialBrand_ExecSummary.pdf\">over 50% of brand reputation</a>&nbsp;comes from online sociability. Being social leads to greater awareness and simply being <i>known</i>.</p> \n<h3>Tell a narrative.</h3> \n<p><a href=\"https://academy.hubspot.com/lessons/the-power-of-storytelling\">Storytelling</a> is an incredibly powerful marketing tactic, whether you’re marketing products or promoting your brand. Why? Because it gives something real for your audience to latch onto.</p> \n<p>Crafting a narrative around your brand humanizes it and gives it depth. And weaving this said narrative into your marketing inherently markets your brand alongside your products or services.</p> \n<p>What should your narrative be about? Anything, as long as it’s true. It can be the narrative of your founder, the tale of how your business had its first product idea, or the little-engine-that-could story of how your small business made it in this big world.</p> \n<p>People like hearing stories about each other. Authenticity is impactful, and it can lead to a big boost in brand awareness.</p> \n<h3>Make sharing easy.</h3> \n<p>Whatever your industry, product offering, or marketing strategies, make it easy for your audience to share your content. This could be blog posts, sponsored content, videos, social media posts, or product pages. It doesn’t matter what it is, as long as it’s shareable.</p> \n<p>Word-of-mouth marketing is the most effective way to establish trust and familiarity among customers. If someone sees that a friend or family member is recommending a product or service, they’ll take notice of that product … and brand. Is this a brand worth exploring? Do they have other great products I can rely on? What are their social accounts like, and what do they talk about?</p> \n<p>If you make it easy to post about your stuff, consumers will raise brand awareness for you by simply clicking “Share”.</p> \n<p><em>Brand awareness is about impact.</em></p> \n<p>It’s about interacting with your audience in ways that don’t only ask for money, participation, or loyalty.</p> \n<p>Imagine if you met a new person who wanted to be your friend. If they asked for any of the above, you’d probably laugh and walk away, right? Not only is that a shallow approach to friendship, but it also leaves no lasting impact on you.</p> \n<p>The same goes for establishing brand awareness among your audience.</p> \n<a></a> \n<h2>How to Increase Brand Awareness</h2> \n<a></a> \n<p>What about expanding your established brand awareness and building on that strong foundation? What can you do as a brand to campaign for awareness and constantly increase it?</p> \n<p>Here are a few campaign ideas to boost your brand awareness:</p> \n<h3>Offer freemium.</h3> \n<a></a> \n<p>Freemium is a business model that offers a basic product or product line for free, only charging for any products deemed premium or enterprise-level. It’s a popular pricing strategy for software companies, like <a href=\"https://www.hubspot.com/products/crm\">HubSpot</a> and <a href=\"https://trello.com/\">Trello</a>.</p> \n<p>Offering a freemium option allows customers to get a taste of your brand and product before making a purchase. It’s a try-before-you-buy opportunity that can, technically, last forever (as opposed to a free trial period that some companies choose).</p> \n<p>It’s common to offer a freemium option with the condition that the brand’s watermark will be shown on any public-facing parts of the product or service. This makes freemium a win-win situation: The consumer gets the product for free, and the brand gets free advertising when consumers use it.&nbsp;</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.typeform.com/\">TypeForm</a> is another great example of this. TypeForm offers a freemium option of its survey software, but customers must include a thank-you page that features the TypeForm logo and message.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/brand%20awareness-typeform.png?width=600&amp;name=brand%20awareness-typeform.png\" alt=\"brand awareness-typeform\" width=\"600\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin: 0px auto;\"></p> \n<p>Depending on your type of business and product offer, Freemium may be the best way to raise awareness of your brand among your audience.</p> \n<h3>Create free content.</h3> \n<p>Nowadays, creating content is easier than ever … which is a good thing because today’s consumers turn to the internet for any and all questions, concerns, and DIY projects.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Content is a fun way to raise awareness of your brand because it’s the easiest way to show personality and share opinions and positioning on issues — two major components that personify and humanize your brand.</p> \n<p>Content doesn’t have to be in written form, either. You can also create videos, infographics, podcasts (which we’ll cover below), and more. Sure, written content like blogs and downloadable guides are arguably the easiest, but they’re definitely not the only option.</p> \n<p>Content doesn’t have to live on just your website, either. Guest posting and sponsored content provide opportunities to get in front of new audiences and diversify the type of content you create.</p> \n<p>If your brand isn’t creating content, you might be missing out on some major brand awareness opportunities. Content provides an amazing way to authentically connect with your audience while getting your brand name in front of people.</p> \n<p>To learn more about content creation, check out our guide here.</p> \n<h3>Sponsor events.</h3> \n<p>How many festivals, concerts, fairs, and exhibitions have you attended? These types of events are typically not possible without the help of brand sponsorships. (Take a look at a t-shirt, koozie, or string backpack you likely grabbed from the event. See any brand names?)&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Sponsoring events is a surefire way to get your brand in front of hundreds, thousands, or millions of people that likely fall into your target audience. From banners to flyers to water bottles, your brand name will be <i>everywhere</i> if you sponsor an event.</p> \n<p>Sponsoring an event also allows you to pin your brand name on an event that matches your personality, interests, and passions, meaning consumers will then associate your brand with that event and its aesthetic and character.</p> \n<p>Consider <a href=\"http://www.redbull.com/\">Red Bull</a>. Red Bull is an energy drink, and without any brand awareness efforts, we’d simply consider it an energy drink. But, thankfully, Red Bull took their marketing to the extreme — literally —&nbsp;by sponsoring extreme sporting events like cliff diving and motocross. They also sponsor <a href=\"https://teamfarang.com/blogs/news/132535299-how-to-get-sponsored-by-red-bull\">athletes</a>. Now, we inherently associate Red Bull with daring and adventurous … and believe that, if we drink it, we can be the same.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/brand%20awareness-red%20bull.jpg?width=600&amp;name=brand%20awareness-red%20bull.jpg\" alt=\"brand awareness-red bull\" width=\"600\" style=\"width: 600px; display: block; margin: 0px auto;\"></p> \n<h3>Give your brand a personality.</h3> \n<p>Treating your brand as a person and defining your narrative are the first steps to giving your brand a personality. The next step would be infusing this personality into your marketing efforts.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>When you market your products and services with personality, you can’t help but boost your brand awareness because your <i>brand</i> will shine right through. Sure, your consumers will take note of the pants or pasta you’re marketing, but they’ll also experience your personality through your advertising.</p> \n<p>This is a great strategy when mixing your traditional marketing campaigns with brand awareness campaigns. They don’t always have to be one in the same, but they definitely can be.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Consider Old Spice. (Did you just picture the man on the horse? I did.) Their advertisements for their hygiene products are overflowing with personality and humor, and they still mention their products throughout. The advertisement not only makes an impact on its viewers, but a mere mention of the “Old Spice man” also sends consumers back to YouTube to watch the commercial … and to the store to buy some deodorant.</p> \n<div class=\"hs-responsive-embed-wrapper hs-responsive-embed\" style=\"margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; display: block; width: 100%; height: auto; position: relative; overflow: hidden; padding: 0; min-width: 256px; margin: 0px auto;\"> \n <div class=\"hs-responsive-embed-inner-wrapper\" style=\"position: relative; overflow: hidden; max-width: 100%; padding-bottom: 56.07%; margin: 0;\">\n  <iframe class=\"hs-responsive-embed-iframe hs-fullwidth-embed\" style=\"position: absolute; top: 0; left: 0; width: 100%; height: 100%; border: none;\" src=\"http://www.youtube.com/embed/owGykVbfgUE\" width=\"560\" height=\"314\" allowfullscreen></iframe>\n </div> \n</div> \n<h3>Produce a podcast.</h3> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.edisonresearch.com/the-infinite-dial-2020/\">More than one-third of Americans 12 and older</a> listen to podcasts regularly. There’s no doubt podcasts play an important role in our lives … and marketing efforts.</p> \n<p>Podcasts used to be a complicated process, only created by those with a studio and fancy microphone. Now, it’s easier than ever to create and release a podcast, and doing so can do wonders for your brand awareness efforts.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Why? Because podcasts, like written or visual content, provide a way to connect with your audience authentically. Instead of blatantly promoting your product or service (which we’ve agreed isn’t the best way to go about boosting brand awareness), podcasts give you the opportunity to educate, inform, entertain, or advise your audience and build trust by doing so.</p> \n<p>Here are some examples of great podcasts produced by brands you know and love:</p> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://itunes.apple.com/us/podcast/lipstories/id1370728732?mt=2\">#LIPSTORIES by Sephora</a></li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://itunes.apple.com/us/podcast/trailblazers-with-walter-isaacson/id1212045046?mt=2\">Trailblazers by Dell</a></li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><a href=\"https://www.hubspot.com/the-growth-show\">The Growth Show by HubSpot</a></li> \n</ul> \n<p>See how these brands have chosen podcast topics that relate to their 1) overall brand message and 2) products or services? Doing this helps them relate the podcast back to their brand and continue to raise awareness, too.</p> \n<p>For more information on podcasts, check out our guide here.</p> \n<p>Building and growing brand awareness is a never-ending process, just as maintaining a friendship or relationship never really ends.</p> \n<p>&nbsp;Boosting your brand awareness through campaigns gives you a chance to dabble in marketing and advertising opportunities you’d otherwise not invest in — meaning new, powerful ways to connect with your audience.</p> \n<a></a> \n<h2>How to Measure Brand Awareness</h2> \n<a></a> \n<p>How do you know if your brand awareness efforts are working? How do you know if you need to change direction, top the competition, or fix a crisis? Just like any other marketing metric, you measure it.</p> \n<p><em>Wait … I thought you said brand awareness couldn’t be measured!</em></p> \n<p>Aha! You’ve been listening. I appreciate that.</p> \n<p>You’re right — brand awareness can’t be measured <i>in the traditional sense</i>. But, you can still review activities and metrics that’ll help you gauge where your brand stands in terms of popularity and consumer awareness.</p> \n<p>Here are a few ways to gauge your brand awareness and learn where you can tweak your efforts:</p> \n<h3>Quantitative Brand Awareness Measures</h3> \n<p>These numbers can help you paint the overall picture of your brand awareness. To measure quantitatively, check out these metrics:</p> \n<a></a> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Direct traffic.</strong> Firstly, direct traffic is the result of people <i>intentionally</i> typing in your URL and visiting your website. Your direct traffic number will tell you how much your marketing is prompting people to visit your website. This is an important metric, as many consumers today discover brands through social media, advertisements, or by typing in keywords related to your brand or product. When consumers go directly to your site, it means they were aware of your brand beforehand.</li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Site traffic numbers. </strong>This number just reflects overall site traffic, which will tell you how much of the general internet population is checking out your content and spending time with your brand. It won’t quite tell you where people came from, but that doesn’t matter, because they’re aware of your brand enough to check it out.</li> \n</ul> \n<a></a> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Social engagement.</strong> Engagement can refer to followers, likes, retweets, comments, and more. It’s a reflection of how many people are aware of your brand and socialize with it, as well as how impactful your content is. For instance, sites like <a href=\"https://sparktoro.com/tools/sparkscore\">Sparktoro</a> can give you a specific score for your Twitter impact.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>Qualitative Brand Awareness Measures</h3> \n<p>This step is where your brand awareness “score” gets a little murky. But these tactics can still help you gauge who and how many people are aware of your brand. To measure qualitatively, try:</p> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Searching Google and setting up </strong><a href=\"https://www.google.com/alerts\"><strong>Google Alerts</strong></a><strong>. </strong>Doing this gets you up to speed with how your brand is being talked about online. It will alert you to any news or mentions by third-party press. As your brand grows, its internet real estate will expand beyond your website, so keep an eye on that.</li> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Social listening.</strong> Social listening is monitoring <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/29437/20-free-social-media-and-brand-monitoring-tools-that-rock.aspx\">social media management tools</a> for organic mentions and engagement. Who’s tagging your brand, mentioning it in comments, or using your hashtag in their posts? These tools can help you discover that. And the more your audience is discussing your brand on social media, the more they’re aware of it.</li> \n</ul> \n<ul> \n <li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>Running brand awareness surveys. </strong>This process involves getting direct feedback from your customers and audience and can be incredibly helpful with not only understanding who knows of your brand but also what they think of it. You can release surveys through <a href=\"https://www.surveymonkey.com/\">SurveyMonkey</a> or <a href=\"https://www.typeform.com/\">TypeForm</a> and share them on social media or directly with your customers. This <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/service/create-a-survey\">guide</a> will help you create and promote them.</li> \n</ul> \n<p>These quantitative and qualitative metrics will help you understand your brand awareness among your audience and the general public. It’ll never be a perfect number, but keeping your pulse on this measure will help influence campaigns and stay connected to your audience. Regardless of how you gauge brand awareness for your company, avoid these <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/mistakes-measure-brand-awareness\">common mistakes</a> when measuring brand awareness.</p> \n<h2>Over to You</h2> \n<p>Brand awareness is a powerful (albeit vague) concept that can have a major impact on your marketing efforts, consumer perception, and revenue.</p> \n<p>Follow these techniques for establishing and building awareness for your brand, and you’ll find yourself with a loyal audience that recognizes your brand among competitors, chooses your products time and time again, and recommends their friends and family do the same.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p>  \n<img src=\"https://track.hubspot.com/__ptq.gif?a=53&amp;k=14&amp;r=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.hubspot.com%2Fmarketing%2Fbrand-awareness&amp;bu=https%253A%252F%252Fblog.hubspot.com%252Fmarketing&amp;bvt=rss\" alt=\"\" width=\"1\" height=\"1\" style=\"min-height:1px!important;width:1px!important;border-width:0!important;margin-top:0!important;margin-bottom:0!important;margin-right:0!important;margin-left:0!important;padding-top:0!important;padding-bottom:0!important;padding-right:0!important;padding-left:0!important; \">","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Nov 2020 13:54:00 +0000","feedId":54,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"e93c9572e4a6842e95d82642a5e93c22","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"adecker@hubspot.com (Allie Decker)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/brand%20awareness-typeform.png?width=600&name=brand%20awareness-typeform.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn98@2020_2/2020/11/14/17-30-25-088_3e669959bf452747.webp","https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/brand%20awareness-red%20bull.jpg?width=600&name=brand%20awareness-red%20bull.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn29@2020_3/2020/11/14/17-30-22-473_849a574ad5fd82df.webp","https://track.hubspot.com/__ptq.gif?a=53&k=14&r=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.hubspot.com%2Fmarketing%2Fbrand-awareness&bu=https%253A%252F%252Fblog.hubspot.com%252Fmarketing&bvt=rss":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn25@2020_3/2020/11/14/17-30-22-475_ca7d91f5884c5efe.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605375018181},{"createdTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","updatedTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","title":"The Ultimate Guide to Marketing Interview Questions From HubSpot's CMO","link":"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/marketing-interview-questions","description":"<p><span style=\"letter-spacing: 0px; background-color: transparent;\">Alright, everyone: I'm about to let you in on a few of my best-kept interviewing secrets. </span></p> \n<p><span style=\"letter-spacing: 0px; background-color: transparent;\">In this post, I'll uncover real questions I use when interviewing candidates for inbound marketing positions and the answers I'm looking for</span><span style=\"letter-spacing: 0px;\">.</span></p> \n<p><span class=\"content-post__body\">These questions are meant to assess candidates not only for their marketing talent, but also for who they are as people.</span></p> \n<p>Keep in mind that the best candidates aren't just qualified to do the job you're trying to hire them for. You want to look for people who are also passionate about marketing, <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/34234/The-HubSpot-Culture-Code-Creating-a-Company-We-Love.aspx\">fit with your culture</a>, and show potential for growth at your company.</p> \n<p>Here's a quick look into my interview approach, followed by 14 excellent interview questions I recommend adapting for your industry and hiring needs.</p> \n<p><span style=\"font-size: 2rem; font-weight: bold;\">My Interview Approach</span></p> \n<p>During interviews, I put a lot of stake into each candidate as an individual. My goal is always to find someone amazing who also has great long-term potential, no matter where they are in their career.</p> \n<p>To uncover this, I like to ask questions that get at the core of who they are, how they think about things specifically, and how they've gotten things done in the real world. I then balance these questions with case-style questions, which usually involve a hypothetical business situation, because they give the candidate an opportunity to show how they think about and work on problems.</p> \n<p>Below is a list of 14 questions that make for an effective marketing job interview, the majority of which I've asked candidates with whom I've personally gotten to meet.</p> \n<p>Keep in mind that I don’t ask <em>all</em> of these questions during a single interview. In fact, one case-style question can evolve into a discussion lasting anywhere from 10 to 30 minutes, so I often only have time to cover two or three questions during one session.</p> \n<p>I also don't limit these questions to the position levels you'll see in each section below. This list is just one reasonable way to organize your job interviews based on the average experience of an intern, coordinator, manager, and director. Depending on the candidate and the needs of the role, a question to a marketing manager candidate might be a good question to ask a marketing coordinator candidate as well.</p> \n<p>Before the interview starts, carefully choose the questions you want to use based on the person's role and background. For an inbound marketing generalist, you could ask any or all of these questions. For someone with a more specific role on a larger inbound marketing team, like a blogger, you could focus only on the questions about blogging and content creation.</p> \n<p>Learn more in the following video, and check out some of my favorite interview questions below.</p> \n<div class=\"video-container-responsive\"> \n <div class=\"hs-responsive-embed hs-responsive-embed-youtube\">\n  <iframe class=\"hs-responsive-embed-iframe\" src=\"http://www.youtube.com/embed/526ooiMvDqg\" width=\"640\" height=\"380\" allowfullscreen></iframe>\n </div> \n</div> \n<h2>14 Interview Questions to Ask Marketing Job Candidates</h2> \n<h3>Case-Style Interview Questions</h3> \n<h4>1. \"Draw a funnel on the whiteboard showing 10,000 visitors, 500 leads, 50 opportunities, and 10 new customers (or any other numbers you think are interesting). Now, pretend you're the CMO for the company, and you have to decide what your marketing team should do to improve on these metrics. Which areas of the funnel would you focus on, and what would you do differently to change these results?\"</h4> \n<p><strong>The Follow-Up:</strong> The follow-up here is simply pushing on the candidate's answers. Typically, they'll pick one part of the funnel to focus on. (And if they don't, I like to push them to do just that.)</p> \n<p>Once they pick one area, I ask them follow-up questions like: \"Which tactics would you think about changing?,\" \"What have you done in your past role that's worked?,\" \"Do you think our company has any unique advantages to get some leverage out of that stage of the funnel?\" I don’t just want them to tell me to \"improve the visitor to lead conversion rate\" -- they need to tell me <em>how</em>.</p> \n<p>If I have time, I'll tell them to pretend they've implemented their ideas, and I'll ask them to go back through the whole funnel and explain how they think each of those initial metrics have changed.</p> \n<p><strong>What to Look For:</strong> Everyone on the marketing team needs to be able to understand how to think about and optimize the funnel. Here's where you assess their thought process, whether they have an intuitive sense of what good and bad conversion rates are, and whether they understand how the funnel steps are connected.</p> \n<p>You'll also gain some insight into whether they understand which different tactics you can use at each step to improve that particular step. (For example, if they say the lead-to-opportunity conversion rate is bad, the right answer is <em>not</em> to write more blog articles.)</p> \n<h4>2. \"We have two potential <a title=\"designs for the homepage\" href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/34006/15-Examples-of-Brilliant-Homepage-Design.aspx\">designs for the homepage</a> of our website, but we don’t know which one to use. The CEO likes one, and the COO likes another. Half the company likes one, and the other half of the company likes the other. Which one should we use?\"</h4> \n<p><strong>The Follow-Up:</strong> This type of question should elicit a ton of questions from the candidate, like who the target audience for the homepage is. If it doesn't, then they're either making up their answer or don't have enough knowledge to address the situation. Follow up by answering their questions with hypotheticals and seeing how they work through the problem.</p> \n<p>If they do pick one side or the other and give you a reason, ask them what the goals are for the homepage. Then, ask them how they'd determine which homepage meets those goals best. From there, tell them that Homepage A performed well based on one of the criteria, and Homepage B performed well based on another one of the criteria. This way, you can assess how they make choices when it's not possible to get data that's 100% conclusive, and they have to choose between two, imperfect variations.</p> \n<p><strong>What to Look For:</strong> While it might seem like this question is all about design, what you're really doing is understanding how candidates approach a conflict of interest. Do they care what each of these people think, or do they go to the data for their answers, such as through A/B testing, user testing, and customer interviews. The best candidates introduce logic and marketing methodology into their answers, while removing opinions. I also like when candidates say you should be constantly tweaking and improving the homepage, rather than always doing a complete redesign every nine or 18 months.</p> \n<h4>3. \"Let's say you have an Excel spreadsheet with 10,000 leads from a few months back -- long enough that those leads' sales cycle has passed. The file contains information about each lead, like their industry, title, company size, and what they did to become a lead (like downloading an ebook). Also in the file is whether they closed as a customer and how much their order was for. Can you use this information to create a lead score? How would you do it?\"</h4> \n<p><em><strong>Note: </strong>I often start this question by simply asking, “How should you create a lead score?” This is how I sort out the people who don't take a data-driven approach. Folks who answer, “You create a lead score by talking to the sales team and then assigning five or ten points to each of the criteria they say they want” are actually wrong. That is not <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/lead-scoring-instructions\">a data-driven approach to lead scoring</a>, and it is way too simplistic to work effectively in most cases.</em></p> \n<p><strong>The Follow-Up:</strong> Most people will answer by talking about \"looking at the data\" and \"sorting the data.\" Push them to tell you how they'd do that in Excel (or another program if they prefer something else). It's not practical to just \"look\" at the data when you have 10,000 rows -- you need to use statistical analysis.</p> \n<p>They also might zone in on one factor, perhaps industry, all alone. If they do that, you should ask them what they would say if the small companies in one industry are good leads, but the big companies in another industry are also good leads? Basically, just keep pushing them until they're at a loss for what to do next.</p> \n<p><strong>What to Look For:</strong> This case-style question is meant to test a candidate's quantitative abilities, and I'd only ask it for people applying for certain marketing roles (like operations). Here, I'm trying to figure out how the candidate thinks about analyzing data and what their sophistication level is around data.</p> \n<p>Most people don't get very far and are either unwilling or unable to look at more than one variable at a time, or understand how to analyze a lot of data in a simple way. At a minimum, you want to find candidates who:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Look at the leads who closed in one group and compare them to the leads who did not close</li> \n <li>Look at <a title=\"multiple variables\" href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/33381/Lead-Scoring-13-Criteria-You-Should-Be-Using-to-Grade-Leads.aspx\">multiple variables</a> at a time</li> \n <li>Use statistical functions in Excel or another program to do that, like summary tables, pivot tables, and so on</li> \n</ul> \n<p>If you find someone who starts making a coherent argument about why you might want to use logistic regression, factor or cluster analysis, actuarial science, or stochastic modeling to figure this out ... refer them to me.</p> \n<h3>Marketing Internship Interview Questions</h3> \n<h4>4. \"What is one of your hobbies? How do you do it?\"</h4> \n<p>This question will help you assess a candidate's ability to explain a concept they know intimately to someone who isn't as familiar with it. If their hobby is training for a marathon, ask them what advice they'd give you if you woke up one day deciding you wanted to train for a marathon. Are they able to communicate it clearly?</p> \n<p>One candidate taught me how to make tagliatelle, which is hand-cut Italian pasta. She gave me the full run-down on how you make the noodles, how you form them and cut them, and which ingredients go into the sauce. She relayed the step-by-step process to me in a way that was very clear and understandable. I felt like I could've gone home and made tagliatelle myself. Not only did this tell me she knows how to convey information clearly, but it also gave me insight into her personality and interests.</p> \n<h4>5. \"What brands do you like or follow on social media and why?\"</h4> \n<p>This is another casual but useful question, as it can tell you both about a candidate's personal interests and how they perceive marketing content on social media. The best answers go further than which companies a candidate likes buying from -- they indicate why he or she trusts certain companies, what about their content strategy appeals to the candidate, and what specifically about those companies the candidate looks up to (and maybe wants to emulate in their own work).</p> \n<p>If you need a candidate to elaborate, follow up by asking them to describe a post from a brand they like or follow, and what made that post so memorable to them.</p> \n<h3>Marketing Coordinator Interview Questions</h3> \n<h4>6. \"What do you read, and how do you consume information?\"</h4> \n<p>Marketing is changing constantly at a rapid pace -- so anyone in a marketing role needs to know how to stay on top of and adapt to these changes. Do they know where to look for industry news? Are they familiar with and subscribed to<a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/top-50-marketing-blogs-on-the-internet\"> top marketing blogs</a>? What do they do when they see a change has taken place, like when Google updates their algorithm?</p> \n<h4>7. \"What's an example of a lead-generating campaign you'd be excited to work on here?\"</h4> \n<p>Not every marketing campaign you run generates the same type or quality of leads. This is what makes this question so interesting. It's a chance for you to see how a marketing candidate thinks about the buyer's journey and what that journey should look like in your company.</p> \n<p>If you do pose this question to a candidate, don't expect him or her to know exactly how your business generates its leads. The ideal answer simply demonstrates an awareness of your customer and perhaps some on-the-spot brainstorming the candidate might be asked to participate in while on the job.</p> \n<p>Expect follow-up questions from the interviewee, too, especially if you pose this question to a more experienced candidate. For example, they might ask how qualified the leads should be, or how leads are scored as a result of this hypothetical campaign. The specific parameters matter less than the follow-up question itself -- a positive sign of an analytical marketer.</p> \n<h4>8. \"What are three components of a successful inbound or digital marketing strategy?\"</h4> \n<p>There's no \"right\" answer to this question -- a digital marketing strategy thrives on more than three things -- but certain answers show the candidate is up to date on how businesses attract and delight their customers today.</p> \n<p>\"A Facebook page,\" for instance, isn't a wrong answer, but it doesn't give you context around how a business would use this page in their marketing strategy. Here are a few sample answers to this interview question that are on the right track:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>A blog with calls to action (CTAs), landing pages for website visitors to download more content, and a defined social media strategy.</li> \n <li>An SEO strategy, website chat, and an analytics tool to track campaign performance.</li> \n <li>&nbsp;Buyer personas, a <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/34212/how-to-create-a-service-level-agreement-sla-for-better-sales-marketing-alignment.aspx\">Marketing and Sales Service Level Agreement</a>, and a customer success strategy.</li> \n</ul> \n<p>You won't learn everything about a candidate from just these terms and phrases. But you should listen for them as the candidate responds -- and expect more sophisticated answers if you pose this question to managers or directors.</p> \n<p>Ultimately, the value you place on each of these inbound marketing components will depend on how important they are to your business and what the candidate would focus on as your employee. Before asking this question to anyone you interview, talk to your team and define your marketing strategy. Otherwise, you won't have an accurate measure on which to evaluate a candidate's answer.</p> \n<h3>Interview Questions for Marketing Manager</h3> \n<h4><strong>9. \"Why do you love marketing?\"</strong></h4> \n<p>Or, \"Which aspects of our business are you passionate about?\" You want to hire someone who's both qualified <em>and</em> has the desire to do the work. Otherwise, why would they work for you instead of the company next door?</p> \n<p>Part of their answer will lie in their body language and enthusiasm. The other part will lie in how concrete their answer is. Get at the details by asking a follow-up question, like: \"Let's say you're at home, kicking around, and doing something related to marketing. What is it that you're doing?\" Perhaps they're reading their five favorite marketing sites, or analyzing traffic patterns of websites for fun, or writing in their personal blog, or optimizing their LinkedIn profile. Whatever it is, you want to be sure they're deeply passionate about the subject matter you'd hire them for.</p> \n<h4><strong>10. \"Between videos, ebooks, blog articles, photos, podcasts, webinars, SlideShare, Facebook, Twitter, LinkedIn, and Pinterest ... there's a lot of potential content our team should produce for inbound marketing. How do we do it all?\"</strong></h4> \n<p>The wisest candidates know you should<em> not</em> do it all, but rather, you should start with the content that's most important to your prospects and customers. They should also have a plan for talking to customers and prospects by way of interviews or surveys to figure out <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/which-social-networks-should-you-focus-on\">which social networks they use</a> and <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/lead-gen-content-ideas\">which types of content</a> they prefer.</p> \n<h4><strong>11. \"Let’s pretend we have very convincing data that shows none of our potential customers use social media. Should we still do it? Why?\"</strong></h4> \n<p>Look for candidates who understand that being successful in social media is important even if your customers aren't there today. Here are a few reasons qualified candidates might cite:</p> \n<ul> \n <ul> \n  <li>Your customers will be there in the future, so you should get started now.</li> \n  <li>You'll gain industry clout. After all, journalists and influencers in your industry are probably using social media -- and it's important for them to follow you <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/31146/Why-You-Need-Social-Media-Followers-Who-Won-t-Ever-Buy.aspx\">even if they don't ever become customers</a>.</li> \n  <li>Social media activity <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/blog/tabid/6307/bid/33662/A-Crystal-Clear-Explanation-of-How-Social-Media-Influences-SEO.aspx\">impacts your organic search presence</a>, helping your content rank higher in search engines.</li> \n  <li>You'll have more control over your online presence.</li> \n  <li>Your competitors are likely using social media.</li> \n  <li>It may cost less to generate customers via social media.</li> \n </ul> \n</ul> \n<h3>Marketing Director Interview Questions</h3> \n<h4><strong>12. \"We have a new product coming out in three months. What would you do to launch it?\"</strong></h4> \n<p>This'll show you how well a candidate understands all the different tactics of inbound marketing and <a title=\"how to tie them together into a holistic plan\" href=\"https://offers.hubspot.com/run-an-inbound-marketing-campaign\">how to tie them together into a holistic plan</a>. It'll also give you insight into how creative they are and whether they can come up with new and interesting ways to do marketing.</p> \n<h4><strong>13. \"Our CEO wants you to evaluate our blog. What would you say?\"</strong></h4> \n<p>Before giving you an answer, the best candidates will come back and ask you about the blog's metrics, how many leads and customers it generates, what the goals are for it, how much you're investing in it, and so on. This is also a great way to test whether they actually prepared for the interview by reading your blog.</p> \n<h4>14. \"What's the main relationship between marketing and sales?\"</h4> \n<p>The <a href=\"https://www.forbes.com/sites/forbesagencycouncil/2017/05/09/are-your-sales-and-marketing-teams-in-a-rivalry/#37219e289e60\">relationship between Marketing and Sales</a> is known for its unrest (Sales wants better leads from Marketing, and Marketing wants Sales to close more, faster).&nbsp;</p> \n<p><span>Similar to question #8, there's no right answer here, but there are answers you should listen for. \"M</span>arketers are the lead generators and salespeople are the lead closers\" isn't necessarily wrong, but the candidate who ends his/her answer here might not be someone who can align both departments around a single, unified approach.</p> \n<p>The best answers describe the responsibilities that Sales and Marketing have to each other, and the duties each commits to as part of this partnership. They have a plan for forging consensus on what makes leads marketing-qualified versus sales-qualified, creating a shared Service Level Agreement with agreed-upon metrics, and using content at different points in the marketing and sales funnel to turn strangers into customers.</p> \n<h2>The Candidate's Follow-Up</h2> \n<p>Most candidates know to follow up with each of their interviewers in the form of a thank-you note or email. But part of my assessment is the depth at which candidates follow up with me.</p> \n<p>The most impressive follow-ups are the thoughtful ones, where candidates call upon details of our discussion to show they're really engaged in the interview process. Perhaps they did more concrete thinking about a specific question I asked, and they send a long email including research on a question they don't think they nailed. Many times, they'll send me a light strategy document with ideas and/or research on something we talked about. These candidates tend to stand out.</p> \n<p>Well, the cat's out of the bag. You'll have to use these marketing interview questions as a basis to create your own, similar questions that are relevant to your industry and hiring needs. Good luck, and happy hiring!</p> \n<p>Want more interview tips? Learn about some of the <a href=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/interview-questions-ask-hiring-managers\">questions candidates should ask</a>&nbsp;hiring managers.</p> \n<p><em></em></p> \n<p><em></em></p>  \n<img src=\"https://track.hubspot.com/__ptq.gif?a=53&amp;k=14&amp;r=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.hubspot.com%2Fmarketing%2Fmarketing-interview-questions&amp;bu=https%253A%252F%252Fblog.hubspot.com%252Fmarketing&amp;bvt=rss\" alt=\"\" width=\"1\" height=\"1\" style=\"min-height:1px!important;width:1px!important;border-width:0!important;margin-top:0!important;margin-bottom:0!important;margin-right:0!important;margin-left:0!important;padding-top:0!important;padding-bottom:0!important;padding-right:0!important;padding-left:0!important; \">","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Nov 2020 02:57:00 +0000","feedId":54,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"61c8d3e3b7407ff0ce43bb6cccc2986e","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"kbodnar@hubspot.com (Kipp Bodnar)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://track.hubspot.com/__ptq.gif?a=53&k=14&r=https%3A%2F%2Fblog.hubspot.com%2Fmarketing%2Fmarketing-interview-questions&bu=https%253A%252F%252Fblog.hubspot.com%252Fmarketing&bvt=rss":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn9@2020_5/2020/11/14/17-30-24-236_186b945187af9256.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605375018184},{"createdTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","updatedTime":"2020-11-15 01:30:18","title":"Data Visualization 101: How to Choose the Right Chart or Graph for Your Data","link":"https://blog.hubspot.com/marketing/types-of-graphs-for-data-visualization","description":"<div class=\"hsg-featured-snippet\"> \n <h2>Types of Charts to Use for Your Data</h2> \n <ul> \n  <li>Column Chart</li> \n  <li>Bar Graph</li> \n  <li>Line Graph</li> \n  <li>Dual Axis Chart</li> \n  <li>Area Chart</li> \n  <li>Stacked Bar Graph</li> \n  <li>Mekko Chart</li> \n  <li>Pie Chart</li> \n  <li>Scatter Plot Chart</li> \n  <li>Bubble Chart</li> \n  <li>Waterfall Chart</li> \n  <li>Funnel Chart</li> \n  <li>Bullet Chart</li> \n  <li>Heat Map</li> \n </ul> \n</div> \n<p>You and I sift through a lot of data for our jobs. Data about website performance, sales performance, product adoption, customer service, marketing campaign results ... the list goes on.</p> \n<p>When you manage multiple <span class=\"content-post__body\"><span class=\"content-post__body\">content assets</span>, such as social media or a blog,</span> with multiple sources of data, it can get overwhelming.What should you be tracking? What actually matters? <a href=\"https://academy.hubspot.com/courses/business-analytics?utm_source=marketing_blog&amp;utm_medium=blog&amp;utm_campaign=business_analytics_course\">How do you visualize and analyze the data</a> so you can extract insights and actionable information?</p> \n<p><span class=\"content-post__body\">M</span>ore importantly, how can you make reporting more efficient when you're busy working on multiple projects at once?</p> \n<p></p> \n<p>One of the struggles that slows down my own reporting and analysis is understanding what types of graphs to use -- and why. That's because choosing the wrong visual aid or simply defaulting to the most common type of data visualization could cause confusion with the viewer or lead to mistaken data interpretation.</p> \n<p>To create charts that clarify and provide the right canvas for analysis, you should first understand the reasons why you might need a chart. In this post, I'll cover five questions to ask yourself when choosing a chart for your data.</p> \n<p>Then, I'll give an overview of 14 different types of charts you have at your disposal.</p> \n<h2>5 Questions to Ask When Deciding Which Type of Chart to Use</h2> \n<h3><span style=\"font-size: 22px; line-height: 1.5; background-color: transparent;\">1. Do you want to compare values?</span></h3> \n<p>Charts are perfect for comparing one or many value sets, and they can easily show the low and high values in the data sets. To create a comparison chart, use these types of graphs:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Column</li> \n <li>Mekko</li> \n <li>Bar</li> \n <li>Pie</li> \n <li>Line</li> \n <li>Scatter Plot</li> \n <li>Bullet</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>2. Do you want to show the composition of something?</h3> \n<p>Use this type of chart to show how individual parts make up the whole of something, such as the device type used for mobile visitors to your website or total sales broken down by sales rep.</p> \n<p>To show composition, use these charts:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Pie</li> \n <li>Stacked Bar</li> \n <li>Mekko</li> \n <li>Stacked Column</li> \n <li>Area</li> \n <li>Waterfall</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>3. Do you want to understand the distribution of your data?</h3> \n<p>Distribution charts help you to understand outliers, the normal tendency, and the range of information in your values.</p> \n<p>Use these charts to show distribution:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Scatter Plot</li> \n <li>Mekko</li> \n <li>Line</li> \n <li>Column</li> \n <li>Bar</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>4. Are you interested in analyzing trends in your data set?</h3> \n<p>If you want to know more information about how a data set performed during a specific time period, there are specific chart types that do extremely well.</p> \n<p>You should choose a:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Line</li> \n <li>Dual-Axis Line</li> \n <li>Column</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>5. Do you want to better understand the relationship between value sets?</h3> \n<p>Relationship charts are suited to showing how one variable relates to one or numerous different variables. You could use this to show how something positively effects, has no effect, or negatively effects another variable.</p> \n<p>When trying to establish the relationship between things, use these charts:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Scatter Plot</li> \n <li>Bubble</li> \n <li>Line</li> \n</ul> \n<h2>14 Different Types of Graphs and Charts for Presenting Data</h2> \n<p>To better understand each chart and how they can be used, here's an overview of each type of chart.</p> \n<h3>1. Column Chart</h3> \n<p>A column chart is used to show a comparison among different items, or it can show a comparison of items over time. You could use this format to see the revenue per landing page or customers by close date.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Customers_by_Close_Date.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Customers_by_Close_Date.png\" alt=\"Column chart - customers by close date\" title=\"Column chart - customers by close date\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Column Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use consistent colors</strong> throughout the chart, selecting accent colors to highlight meaningful data points or changes over time.</li> \n <li><strong>Use horizontal labels</strong> to improve readability.</li> \n <li><strong>Start the y-axis at 0</strong> to appropriately reflect the values in your graph.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>2. Bar Graph</h3> \n<p>A bar graph, basically a horizontal column chart, should be used to avoid clutter when one data label is long or if you have more than 10 items to compare. This type of visualization can also be used to display negative numbers.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Customers_by_Role.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Customers_by_Role.png\" alt=\"Bar chart - customers by role\" title=\"Bar chart - customers by role\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Bar Graphs:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use consistent colors</strong> throughout the chart, selecting accent colors to highlight meaningful data points or changes over time.</li> \n <li><strong>Use horizontal labels</strong> to improve readability.</li> \n <li><strong>Start the y-axis at 0</strong> to appropriately reflect the values in your graph.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>3. Line Graph</h3> \n<p>A line graph reveals trends or progress over time and can be used to show many different categories of data. You should use it when you chart a continuous data set.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Average_Days_to_Close_by_Date_Became_SQL.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Average_Days_to_Close_by_Date_Became_SQL.png\" alt=\"Line chart - avg days to close\" title=\"Line chart - avg days to close\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Line Graphs:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use solid lines only.</strong></li> \n <li><strong>Don't plot more than four lines</strong> to avoid visual distractions.</li> \n <li><strong>Use the right height</strong> so the lines take up roughly 2/3 of the y-axis' height.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>4. Dual Axis Chart</h3> \n<p>A dual axis chart allows you to plot data using two y-axes and a shared x-axis. It's used with three data sets, one of which is based on a continuous set of data and another which is better suited to being grouped by category. This should be used to visualize a correlation or the lack thereof between these three data sets.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Revenue_by_Number_of_New_Customers_by_Date.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Revenue_by_Number_of_New_Customers_by_Date.png\" alt=\"Dual axis chart - revenue by new customers\" title=\"Dual axis chart - revenue by new customers\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Dual Axis Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use the y-axis on the left side for the primary variable</strong> because brains are naturally inclined to look left first.</li> \n <li><strong>Use different graphing styles</strong> to illustrate the two data sets, as illustrated above.</li> \n <li><strong>Choose contrasting colors</strong> for the two data sets.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>5. Area Chart</h3> \n<p>An area chart is basically a line chart, but the space between the x-axis and the line is filled with a color or pattern. It is useful for showing part-to-whole relations, such as showing individual sales reps' contribution to total sales for a year. It helps you analyze both overall and individual trend information.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Users_by_Creation_Date_and_Life_Cycle_Stage_1.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Users_by_Creation_Date_and_Life_Cycle_Stage_1.png\" alt=\"Area chart - users by lifecycle stage\" title=\"Area chart - users by lifecycle stage\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Area Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use transparent colors</strong> so information isn't obscured in the background.</li> \n <li><strong>Don't display more than four categories</strong> to avoid clutter.</li> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\">Organize highly variable data at the top of the chart to <strong>make it easy to read.</strong></span></li> \n</ul> \n<h3>6. Stacked Bar Chart</h3> \n<p>This should be used to compare many different items and show the composition of each item being compared.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_When_MQLs_become_SQLs.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_When_MQLs_become_SQLs.png\" alt=\"Stacked bar chart - mqls to sqls\" title=\"Stacked bar chart - mqls to sqls\" width=\"669\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Stacked Bar Graphs:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\">Best used to illustrate <strong>part-to-whole relationships.</strong></span></li> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\">Use <strong>contrasting colors</strong> for greater clarity.</span></li> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\"><strong>Make chart scale large enough</strong> to view group sizes in relation to one another.</span></li> \n</ul> \n<h3>7. Mekko Chart</h3> \n<p>Also known as a marimekko chart, this type of graph can compare values, measure each one's composition, and show how your data is distributed across each one.</p> \n<p>It's similar to a stacked bar, except the mekko's x-axis is used to capture another dimension of your values --&nbsp;<span>rather than time progression, like column charts often do.</span> In the graphic below, the x-axis compares each city to one another.</p> \n<p><img src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Bar%20Mekko%20Chart.png?width=600&amp;name=Bar%20Mekko%20Chart.png\" alt=\"Mekko chart - world's largest asset managers\" title=\"Mekko chart - world's largest asset managers\" width=\"600\" style=\"width: 600px;\"></p> \n<p style=\"text-align: center;\"><span style=\"font-size: 13px;\">Image via <a href=\"https://www.mekkographics.com/portfolio-item/deal-profile/\">Mekko Graphics</a></span></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Mekko Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Vary you bar heights</strong> if the portion size is an important point of comparison.</li> \n <li><strong>Don't include too many composite values</strong> within each bar. you might want to reevaluate how to present your data if you have a lot.&nbsp;</li> \n <li><strong>Order your bars</strong> from left to right in such a way that exposes a relevant trend or message.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>8. Pie Chart</h3> \n<p>A pie chart shows a static number and how categories represent part of a whole -- the composition of something. A pie chart represents numbers in percentages, and the total sum of all segments needs to equal 100%.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Customers_by_Role_in_Company.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Customers_by_Role_in_Company.png\" alt=\"Pie chart - customers by role\" title=\"Pie chart - customers by role\" width=\"669\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Pie Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Don't illustrate too many categories</strong> to ensure differentiation between slices.</li> \n <li>Ensure that the slice values <strong>add up to 100%.</strong></li> \n <li><strong>Order slices</strong> according to their size.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>9. Scatter Plot Chart</h3> \n<p>A scatter plot or scattergram chart will show the relationship between two different variables or it can reveal the distribution trends. It should be used when there are many different data points, and you want to highlight similarities in the data set. This is useful when looking for outliers or for understanding the distribution of your data.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/scatter-plot-customer-happines.png?width=669&amp;name=scatter-plot-customer-happines.png\" alt=\"Scatter plot chart - customer happiness by response time\" title=\"Scatter plot chart - customer happiness by response time\" width=\"669\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Scatter Plots:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Include more variables</strong>, such as different sizes, to incorporate more data.</li> \n <li><strong>Start y-axis at 0</strong> to represent data accurately.</li> \n <li>If you use <strong>trend lines</strong>, only use a maximum of two to make your plot easy to understand.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>10. Bubble Chart</h3> \n<p>A bubble chart is similar to a scatter plot in that it can show distribution or relationship. There is a third data set, which is indicated by the size of the bubble or circle.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/bubble-chart.png?width=669&amp;name=bubble-chart.png\" alt=\"Bubble chart - hours spent online by age and gender\" title=\"Bubble chart - hours spent online by age and gender\" width=\"669\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Bubble Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Scale bubbles according to area</strong>, not diameter.</li> \n <li>Make sure <strong>labels are clear and visible.</strong></li> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\"><strong>Use circular shapes</strong> only.</span></li> \n</ul> \n<h3>11. Waterfall Chart</h3> \n<p>A waterfall chart should be used to show how an initial value is affected by intermediate values -- either positive or negative -- and resulted in a final value. This should be used to reveal the composition of a number. An example of this would be to showcase how overall company revenue is influenced by different departments and leads to a specific profit number.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/waterfall-chart.png?width=669&amp;name=waterfall-chart.png\" alt=\"Waterfall chart - product profit analysis\" title=\"Waterfall chart - product profit analysis\" width=\"669\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<p style=\"text-align: center;\"><span style=\"font-size: 12px;\"><em>Chart via <a href=\"http://baansconsulting.com/?p=2303\">Baans Consulting</a></em></span></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for <span class=\"content-post__body\">Waterfall Charts</span>:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use contrasting colors</strong> to highlight differences in data sets.</li> \n <li>Choose warm colors to indicate increases and cool colors to indicate decreases.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>12. Funnel Chart</h3> \n<p>A funnel chart shows a series of steps and the completion rate for each step. This can be used to track the sales process or the conversion rate across a series of pages or steps.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/DataHero_Marketing_Funnel.png?width=669&amp;name=DataHero_Marketing_Funnel.png\" alt=\"Funnel chart - marketing funnel process\" title=\"Funnel chart - marketing funnel process\" width=\"669\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Funnel Charts:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Scale the size of each section</strong> to accurately reflect the size of the data set.</li> \n <li>Use <strong>contrasting colors</strong> or <strong>one color</strong> in gradating hues, from darkest to lightest as the size of the funnel decreases.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>13. Bullet Graph</h3> \n<p>A bullet graph reveals progress toward a goal, compares this to another measure, and provides context in the form of a rating or performance.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/bullet-graph3.png?width=500&amp;name=bullet-graph3.png\" alt=\"Bullet graph - new customers\" title=\"Bullet graph - new customers\" width=\"500\" style=\"display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; width: 500px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Bullet Graphs:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use contrasting colors</strong> to highlight how the data is progressing.</li> \n <li><strong>Use one color</strong> in different shades to gauge progress.</li> \n</ul> \n<h3>14. Heat Map</h3> \n<p>A heat map shows the relationship between two items and provides rating information, such as high to low or poor to excellent. The rating information is displayed using varying colors or saturation.</p> \n<p><img class=\"alignCenter shadow\" src=\"https://blog.hubspot.com/hs-fs/hubfs/Agency_Post/Blog_Images/heat-map-chart.png?width=669&amp;name=heat-map-chart.png\" alt=\"Heat map chart - highest degree vs. class identification\" width=\"669\" title=\"Heat map chart - highest degree vs. class identification\" style=\"width: 669px;\"></p> \n<h4>Design Best Practices for Heat Map:</h4> \n<ul> \n <li><strong>Use a </strong><span class=\"content-post__body\"><strong>basic and clear map outline</strong> to avoid distracting from the data.</span></li> \n <li><span class=\"content-post__body\"><strong>Use a single color</strong> in varying shades to show changes in data.</span></li> \n <li><strong>Avoid using multiple patterns.</strong></li> \n</ul>  \n<img
